<p>The <b>Vancouver Canucks</b> are a professional <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a> team based in <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>. They are members of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Division_(NHL)" title="Pacific Division (NHL)">Pacific Division</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Conference_(NHL)" title="Western Conference (NHL)">Western Conference</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a> (NHL). The Canucks play their home games at <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Arena" title="Rogers Arena">Rogers Arena</a>, formerly known as General Motors Place, which has an official capacity of 18,860. <a href="/wiki/Henrik_Sedin" title="Henrik Sedin">Henrik Sedin</a> is currently the captain of the team, <a href="/wiki/Willie_Desjardins" title="Willie Desjardins">Willie Desjardins</a> is the head coach and Jim Benning is the general manager.</p>
<p>The Canucks joined the league in <a href="/wiki/1970%E2%80%9371_NHL_season" title="1970–71 NHL season">1970</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Expansion_team" title="Expansion team">expansion team</a> along with the <a href="/wiki/Buffalo_Sabres" title="Buffalo Sabres">Buffalo Sabres</a>. In its NHL history, the team has advanced to the <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="Stanley Cup Finals">Stanley Cup Finals</a> three times, losing to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Islanders" title="New York Islanders">New York Islanders</a> in <a href="/wiki/1982_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1982 Stanley Cup Finals">1982</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Rangers" title="New York Rangers">New York Rangers</a> in <a href="/wiki/1994_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1994 Stanley Cup Finals">1994</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Bruins" title="Boston Bruins">Boston Bruins</a> in <a href="/wiki/2011_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="2011 Stanley Cup Finals">2011</a>. They have won the <a href="/wiki/Presidents%27_Trophy" title="Presidents' Trophy">Presidents' Trophy</a> in back-to-back seasons as the team with the league's best regular season record in the <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9311_NHL_season" title="2010–11 NHL season">2010–11</a> and <a href="/wiki/2011%E2%80%9312_NHL_season" title="2011–12 NHL season">2011–12</a> seasons. They won three division titles as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Smythe_Division" title="Smythe Division">Smythe Division</a> from 1974 to 1993, and seven titles as a member of the Northwest Division from 1998 to 2013.</p>
<p>The Canucks have retired four players' jerseys in their history — <a href="/wiki/Stan_Smyl" title="Stan Smyl">Stan Smyl</a> (12), <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Linden" title="Trevor Linden">Trevor Linden</a> (16), <a href="/wiki/Markus_N%C3%A4slund" title="Markus Näslund">Markus Naslund</a> (19) and <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Bure" title="Pavel Bure">Pavel Bure</a> (10); all but Bure have served as team captain. Smyl has the distinction of being the only Canuck to have his jersey number retired at their former arena, the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Coliseum" title="Pacific Coliseum">Pacific Coliseum</a>, as well as the only Canuck to play his entire career with the team upon retiring it.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hockey_background_in_Vancouver">Hockey background in Vancouver</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vancouver_Canucks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Hockey background in Vancouver">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Vancouver became home to a professional ice hockey team for the first time in 1911 when Patrick brothers <a href="/wiki/Frank_Patrick_(ice_hockey)" title="Frank Patrick (ice hockey)">Frank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lester_Patrick" title="Lester Patrick">Lester</a> established the <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Millionaires" title="Vancouver Millionaires">Vancouver Millionaires</a>, one of three teams in the new <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Coast_Hockey_Association" title="Pacific Coast Hockey Association">Pacific Coast Hockey Association</a>. To accommodate the Millionaires, the Patrick brothers directed the building of the <a href="/wiki/Denman_Arena" title="Denman Arena">Denman Arena</a>, which was known at the time as the world's largest artificial ice rink (it burned down in 1936).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The Millionaires played for the <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup" title="Stanley Cup">Stanley Cup</a> five times, winning over the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Senators_(original)" title="Ottawa Senators (original)">Ottawa Senators</a> in <a href="/wiki/1915_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1915 Stanley Cup Finals">1915</a> on home ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M.C3.BCller2005_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-M.C3.BCller2005-3">[3]</a></sup> It marked the first time the Stanley Cup was won by a West Coast team in the trophy's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M.C3.BCller2005_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-M.C3.BCller2005-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the Millionaires disbanded following the 1925–26 season, Vancouver was home to only <a href="/wiki/Minor_league" title="Minor league">minor league</a> teams for many years. Most notably, the present-day Canucks' minor league predecessor (also known as the <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Canucks_(WHL)" title="Vancouver Canucks (WHL)">Vancouver Canucks</a>), played from 1945 to 1970 in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Coast_Hockey_League" title="Pacific Coast Hockey League">Pacific Coast Hockey League</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Hockey_League_(minor_pro)" title="Western Hockey League (minor pro)">Western Hockey League</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Team_history">Team history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vancouver_Canucks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Team history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1967.E2.80.931970:_NHL_application">1967–1970: NHL application</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vancouver_Canucks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1967–1970: NHL application">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With the intention of attracting an NHL franchise, Vancouver began the construction of a new modern arena, the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Coliseum" title="Pacific Coliseum">Pacific Coliseum</a>, in 1967.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The WHL's Canucks were playing in a small arena at the time, the <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Forum" title="Vancouver Forum">Vancouver Forum</a>, situated on the same <a href="/wiki/Pacific_National_Exhibition" title="Pacific National Exhibition">Pacific National Exhibition</a> grounds as the Coliseum. Meanwhile, a Vancouver group led by WHL Canucks owner and former Vancouver mayor <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Hume" title="Frederick Hume">Fred Hume</a> made a bid to be one of the six teams due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1967_NHL_Expansion" title="1967 NHL Expansion">join the league in 1967</a>, but the NHL rejected their application.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ImlachYoung1986_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-ImlachYoung1986-5">[5]</a></sup> Bid leader <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cyrus_McLean&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cyrus McLean (page does not exist)">Cyrus McLean</a> called the denial a "cooked-up deal", referring to several biases that factored against them. Speculation long abounded afterwards that the bid was hindered by <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Maple_Leafs" title="Toronto Maple Leafs">Toronto Maple Leafs</a> president <a href="/wiki/Stafford_Smythe" title="Stafford Smythe">Stafford Smythe</a>; after a failed Vancouver-based business deal, he was quoted as saying that the city would not get a NHL franchise in his lifetime.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Additionally, along with the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Canadiens" title="Montreal Canadiens">Montreal Canadiens</a>, Smythe purportedly did not wish to split <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Canadian Broadcasting Corporation">Canadian Broadcasting Corporation</a> (CBC) hockey revenues three ways rather than two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> There were reports at the time, however, that the group had made a very weak proposal in expectation that Vancouver was a lock for one of the new franchises.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Less than a year later, the <a href="/wiki/California_Golden_Seals" title="California Golden Seals">Oakland Seals</a> were in financial difficulty and having trouble drawing fans. An apparent deal was in place to move the team to Vancouver, but the NHL did not want to see one of their franchises from the expansion of 1967 move so quickly and vetoed the deal. In exchange for avoiding a lawsuit, the NHL promised Vancouver would get a team in the next expansion. Another group, headed by <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a> entrepreneur <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tom_Scallen" title="Tom Scallen">Tom Scallen</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> made a new presentation and was awarded an expansion franchise for the price of $6 million (three times the cost in <a href="/wiki/1967%E2%80%9368_NHL_season" title="1967–68 NHL season">1967</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The new ownership group purchased the WHL Canucks, and brought the team into the league with the <a href="/wiki/Buffalo_Sabres" title="Buffalo Sabres">Buffalo Sabres</a> as expansion teams for the <a href="/wiki/1970%E2%80%9371_NHL_season" title="1970–71 NHL season">1970–71 season</a>. Scallen retained a portion of the WHL Canucks roster such as <a href="/wiki/John_Arbour" title="John Arbour">John Arbour</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Gardner_(ice_hockey)" title="George Gardner (ice hockey)">George Gardner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Len_Lunde" title="Len Lunde">Len Lunde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marc_Reaume" title="Marc Reaume">Marc Reaume</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ted_Taylor_(ice_hockey)" title="Ted Taylor (ice hockey)">Ted Taylor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Murray_Hall_(ice_hockey)" title="Murray Hall (ice hockey)">Murray Hall</a>. The rest of the club would be built through an expansion draft.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970.E2.80.931982:_Early_years">1970–1982: Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vancouver_Canucks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1970–1982: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To fill the Canucks' roster for their inaugural season, the league held an <a href="/wiki/1970_NHL_Expansion_Draft" title="1970 NHL Expansion Draft">Expansion Draft</a> in the preceding summer. A draft lottery was held on June 9, 1970, determining who between the Canucks and Sabres would get the first selection in the Expansion Draft, as well as the <a href="/wiki/1970_NHL_Amateur_Draft" title="1970 NHL Amateur Draft">1970 NHL Amateur Draft</a>; the Sabres won both spins. With his first selection in the Expansion Draft, Canucks General Manager <a href="/wiki/Bud_Poile" title="Bud Poile">Bud Poile</a> chose defenceman <a href="/wiki/Gary_Doak" title="Gary Doak">Gary Doak</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Among the other players chosen by Vancouver were centre <a href="/wiki/Orland_Kurtenbach" title="Orland Kurtenbach">Orland Kurtenbach</a>, who was named the Canucks' first captain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mason2003_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mason2003-9">[9]</a></sup> as well as defenceman <a href="/wiki/Pat_Quinn_(ice_hockey)" title="Pat Quinn (ice hockey)">Pat Quinn</a>, who later became the team's general manager and coach in the 1990s. Two days later, on June 11, 1970, the Canucks made defenceman <a href="/wiki/Dale_Tallon" title="Dale Tallon">Dale Tallon</a> their first-ever Amateur Draft selection. Tallon played three seasons with the club before being traded away to the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Blackhawks" title="Chicago Blackhawks">Chicago Black Hawks</a>. By comparison, the Sabres chose centre <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Perreault" title="Gilbert Perreault">Gilbert Perreault</a> with the first overall selection they won from the lottery; Perreault went on to become a nine-time <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League_All-Star_Game" title="National Hockey League All-Star Game">All-Star</a> and member of the <a href="/wiki/Hockey_Hall_of_Fame" title="Hockey Hall of Fame">Hockey Hall of Fame</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stan_Smyl_eagle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Stan_Smyl_eagle.jpg/225px-Stan_Smyl_eagle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Stan_Smyl_eagle.jpg/338px-Stan_Smyl_eagle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Stan_Smyl_eagle.jpg/450px-Stan_Smyl_eagle.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stan_Smyl_eagle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eagle sculpture featuring Stan Smyl, the team's longest serving captain.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With the Canucks' roster set, the team played its inaugural game against the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Kings" title="Los Angeles Kings">Los Angeles Kings</a> on October 9, 1970. They lost the contest 3–1; defenceman <a href="/wiki/Barry_Wilkins" title="Barry Wilkins">Barry Wilkins</a> scored the Canucks' lone goal in the game and first in franchise history, a backhander against goaltender <a href="/wiki/Denis_DeJordy" title="Denis DeJordy">Denis DeJordy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bedall2004_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bedall2004-11">[11]</a></sup> Two days later, the squad recorded the first win in franchise history, a 5–3 victory over the Toronto Maple Leafs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Canucks struggled in their early years, failing to make the playoffs in their first four seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup> Placed in the competitive <a href="/wiki/East_Division_(NHL)" title="East Division (NHL)">East Division</a>, Poile assembled a core of players during this period led by Kurtenbach that included defencemen Tallon and <a href="/wiki/Jocelyn_Guevremont" title="Jocelyn Guevremont">Jocelyn Guevremont</a>, as well as wingers <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Boudrias" title="André Boudrias">Andre Boudrias</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Ververgaert" title="Dennis Ververgaert">Dennis Ververgaert</a>. Boudrias emerged as the team's leading point-scorer in four of their first five seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seasons_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-seasons-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prior to the <a href="/wiki/1974%E2%80%9375_NHL_season" title="1974–75 NHL season">1974–75 season</a>, Scallen and his ownership group from Minnesota sold the team to local media mogul <a href="/wiki/Frank_Griffiths" title="Frank Griffiths">Frank Griffiths</a> for $9 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Also in the summer of 1974, the Canucks were re-aligned within the league and placed in the new <a href="/wiki/Smythe_Division" title="Smythe Division">Smythe Division</a>. They responded with their first winning record (38 wins, 32 losses and 10 ties), finishing first in the Division with 86 points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seasons_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-seasons-14">[14]</a></sup> Making their debut in the <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="Stanley Cup playoffs">Stanley Cup playoffs</a>, the Canucks lost the opening series of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1975_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="1975 Stanley Cup playoffs">1975 post-season</a> in five games to the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Canadiens" title="Montreal Canadiens">Montreal Canadiens</a>. Head Coach and General Manager <a href="/wiki/Phil_Maloney" title="Phil Maloney">Phil Maloney</a> (the third GM in team history after Poile and <a href="/wiki/Hal_Laycoe" title="Hal Laycoe">Hal Laycoe</a>) recalled the importance of a successful season for the Canucks in that year specifically, as the rival league <a href="/wiki/World_Hockey_Association" title="World Hockey Association">World Hockey Association</a> (WHA) had established another major professional team in the city, the <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Blazers" title="Vancouver Blazers">Vancouver Blazers</a>. Competing for the same hockey market, the Canucks emerged over the Blazers as the latter relocated to <a href="/wiki/Calgary" title="Calgary">Calgary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, the <a href="/wiki/1975%E2%80%9376_WHA_season" title="1975–76 WHA season">following season</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The Canucks posted a second consecutive winning record and made the playoffs in <a href="/wiki/1975%E2%80%9376_NHL_season" title="1975–76 NHL season">1975–76</a>, but lost to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Islanders" title="New York Islanders">New York Islanders</a> in a two-game preliminary series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Canucks missed the playoffs in the two seasons thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Kurtenbach had since retired and assumed a coaching position with Vancouver. His departure as a player marked the beginning of a seven-year period in which the Canucks had four different captains – Boudrias, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Oddleifson" title="Chris Oddleifson">Chris Oddleifson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Don_Lever" title="Don Lever">Don Lever</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kevin_McCarthy_(ice_hockey)" title="Kevin McCarthy (ice hockey)">Kevin McCarthy</a>. Following their post-season loss to the Islanders in 1976, Vancouver did not have another winning season for 16 years, though they made the playoffs nine times in that span.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seasons_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-seasons-14">[14]</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/1976%E2%80%9377_NHL_season" title="1976–77 NHL season">1976–77 season</a>, Maloney was replaced as general manager by <a href="/wiki/Jake_Milford" title="Jake Milford">Jake Milford</a>, who acquired such players as <a href="/wiki/Stan_Smyl" title="Stan Smyl">Stan Smyl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Gradin" title="Thomas Gradin">Thomas Gradin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Brodeur" title="Richard Brodeur">Richard Brodeur</a>, a core that would lead the team throughout the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1982_Stanley_Cup_run">1982 Stanley Cup run</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vancouver_Canucks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1982 Stanley Cup run">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/1981%E2%80%9382_Vancouver_Canucks_season" title="1981–82 Vancouver Canucks season">1981–82 Vancouver Canucks season</a> and <a href="/wiki/1982_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1982 Stanley Cup Finals">1982 Stanley Cup Finals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roger_Neilson_Statue_2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Roger_Neilson_Statue_2011.jpg/300px-Roger_Neilson_Statue_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Roger_Neilson_Statue_2011.jpg/450px-Roger_Neilson_Statue_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Roger_Neilson_Statue_2011.jpg/600px-Roger_Neilson_Statue_2011.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roger_Neilson_Statue_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A statue of coach <a href="/wiki/Roger_Neilson" title="Roger Neilson">Roger Neilson</a> outside of <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Arena" title="Rogers Arena">Rogers Arena</a>, commemorating the 1982 Stanley Cup run.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Canucks made their first significant playoff impact in the <a href="/wiki/1982_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="1982 Stanley Cup playoffs">post-season of 1982</a>. In their previous five playoff appearances, the team had failed to win a single series. Though the Canucks finished three games under a .500 win percentage in the <a href="/wiki/1981%E2%80%9382_NHL_season" title="1981–82 NHL season">1981–82 regular season</a>, they began gaining momentum by finishing the campaign on a nine-game unbeaten streak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Smyl emerged as the club's leader, replacing McCarthy as captain after the latter was sidelined with an injury late in the season (he would retain that position for a team-record eight years).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-longest_captaincy_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-longest_captaincy-20">[20]</a></sup> Continuing their success in the playoffs, the Canucks made the <a href="/wiki/1982_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1982 Stanley Cup Finals">Stanley Cup Finals</a> with a combined 11–2 record in series against the <a href="/wiki/Calgary_Flames" title="Calgary Flames">Calgary Flames</a>, Los Angeles Kings, and Chicago Black Hawks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup> Despite having a losing regular season record, Vancouver had home ice advantage in the first series, having finished second in the Smythe Division to the <a href="/wiki/Edmonton_Oilers" title="Edmonton Oilers">Edmonton Oilers</a>. The Canucks also had home ice advantage during the second round series against the Kings, who upset the Oilers in the first round.</p>
<p>Late in Game 2 of the Conference Finals in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, Vancouver Interim Head Coach <a href="/wiki/Roger_Neilson" title="Roger Neilson">Roger Neilson</a>, frustrated with what he felt was the poor officiating in the game, placed a white towel on the end of a hockey stick and held it up in a gesture mocking surrender (waving the <a href="/wiki/White_flag" title="White flag">white flag</a>). The players on the Canucks' bench followed suit. When the series shifted to Vancouver for the next two games, the team's fans cheered them on by <a href="/wiki/Rally_towel" title="Rally towel">waving white towels</a> above their heads. The habit stuck, becoming an original Canuck fan tradition now seen across the league and in other sports, known as "<a href="/wiki/Towel_Power" title="Towel Power">Towel Power</a>." The Canucks proceeded to win the series in five games, making it to the Stanley Cup Finals for the first time in their history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Towel_Power" title="Towel Power">Towel Power</a></div>
<p>Entering the Finals against the New York Islanders, the Canucks were the first team from Western Canada to play for the Stanley Cup in 56 years, when the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Cougars" title="Victoria Cougars">Victoria Cougars</a> reached the <a href="/wiki/1926_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1926 Stanley Cup Finals">1926 Stanley Cup Finals</a>. It also marked the first ever coast-to-coast Stanley Cup Finals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Competing against the Islanders – the <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup" title="Stanley Cup">Stanley Cup</a> champions of the previous two years, who had finished with 41 points more than Vancouver in the regular season standings – Vancouver took the first game to <a href="/wiki/Overtime_(ice_hockey)" title="Overtime (ice hockey)">overtime</a>. In the final minute of the extra period, Canucks defenceman and fan favourite <a href="/wiki/Harold_Snepsts" title="Harold Snepsts">Harold Snepsts</a> gave the puck away with an errant pass from behind his net, leading to a <a href="/wiki/Mike_Bossy" title="Mike Bossy">Mike Bossy</a> goal. Like the first game, the Canucks held a 3–2 lead after the first two periods in the second game, but were not able to keep their lead, and lost 6–4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The Canucks were unable to complete their <a href="/wiki/Cinderella_(sports)" title="Cinderella (sports)">Cinderella</a> run and were swept, losing their next two games by 3–0 and 3–1 scores. The 1982 playoffs proved to be the last year in which Vancouver won a playoff series until <a href="/wiki/1992_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="1992 Stanley Cup playoffs">1992</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1982.E2.80.931994:_Decline_and_resurgence">1982–1994: Decline and resurgence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vancouver_Canucks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1982–1994: Decline and resurgence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After their improbable Stanley Cup run, the Canucks slipped back into mediocrity for the rest of the 1980s, making the playoffs only four times for the rest of the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup> Notable players that joined the Canucks' core following the 1982 playoffs included offensively-skilled forwards <a href="/wiki/Patrik_Sundstr%C3%B6m" title="Patrik Sundström">Patrik Sundstrom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tony_Tanti" title="Tony Tanti">Tony Tanti</a>. Beginning in <a href="/wiki/1983%E2%80%9384_NHL_season" title="1983–84 NHL season">1983–84</a>, the Canucks' scoring title was held by either Sundstrom or Tanti for four of the next five seasons. For most of the second half of the 1980s, the Canucks competed with the Los Angeles Kings for the final playoff spot in the Smythe Division. The years in which they qualified, the team was eliminated by the Edmonton Oilers (in <a href="/wiki/1985%E2%80%9386_NHL_season" title="1985–86 NHL season">1985–86</a>) or the Calgary Flames (in <a href="/wiki/1982%E2%80%9383_NHL_season" title="1982–83 NHL season">1982–83</a>, <a href="/wiki/1983%E2%80%9384_NHL_season" title="1983–84 NHL season">1983–84</a> and Flames championship season of <a href="/wiki/1989_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1989 Stanley Cup Finals">1989</a>, which was decided in Game 7), both division rivals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pavel_Bure_in_Canucks_uniform.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1890" data-file-width="1339" height="339" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Pavel_Bure_in_Canucks_uniform.jpg/240px-Pavel_Bure_in_Canucks_uniform.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Pavel_Bure_in_Canucks_uniform.jpg/360px-Pavel_Bure_in_Canucks_uniform.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Pavel_Bure_in_Canucks_uniform.jpg/480px-Pavel_Bure_in_Canucks_uniform.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pavel_Bure_in_Canucks_uniform.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pavel_Bure" title="Pavel Bure">Pavel Bure</a>, nicknamed "The Russian Rocket", became the first Canuck to win the <a href="/wiki/Calder_Memorial_Trophy" title="Calder Memorial Trophy">Calder Memorial Trophy</a> in <a href="/wiki/1991%E2%80%9392_NHL_season" title="1991–92 NHL season">1992</a> and is the only sixty-goal scorer in team history. He is regarded as the team's first superstar player.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following Milford's tenure as general manager from 1977 to 1982, the position was held by <a href="/wiki/Harry_Neale" title="Harry Neale">Harry Neale</a> for three years, then <a href="/wiki/Jack_Gordon_(ice_hockey)" title="Jack Gordon (ice hockey)">Jack Gordon</a> for two. The latter was responsible for trading away <a href="/wiki/Power_forward_(ice_hockey)" title="Power forward (ice hockey)">power forward</a> <a href="/wiki/Cam_Neely" title="Cam Neely">Cam Neely</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Bruins" title="Boston Bruins">Boston Bruins</a> in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In addition to Neely, the Canucks gave up their 1987 first round draft pick, with which the Bruins chose <a href="/wiki/Glen_Wesley" title="Glen Wesley">Glen Wesley</a>, and in return acquired centre <a href="/wiki/Barry_Pederson" title="Barry Pederson">Barry Pederson</a>. While Pederson collected back-to-back 70-point seasons with the Canucks in his first two seasons after the trade, he was traded away to the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Penguins" title="Pittsburgh Penguins">Pittsburgh Penguins</a> in 1989 as his performance quickly declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Neely went on to have a Hall of Fame career with the Bruins, recording three 50-goal seasons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and Wesley had a solid 20-year career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the installation of former Canucks defenceman Pat Quinn as general manager in the summer of 1987,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FischlerFischler2003_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FischlerFischler2003-28">[28]</a></sup> the team underwent an immediate rebuilding process, trading away core veterans for younger prospects and players. Among the more key transactions was a deal with the <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Devils" title="New Jersey Devils">New Jersey Devils</a>, in which Sundstrom was traded away in exchange for winger <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greg_Adams_(ice_hockey_b._1963)" title="Greg Adams (ice hockey b. 1963)">Greg Adams</a> and goaltender <a href="/wiki/Kirk_McLean" title="Kirk McLean">Kirk McLean</a>. In addition to Quinn's trades, the team improved through the draft route with two selections, in particular. With the second overall selection in the <a href="/wiki/1988_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="1988 NHL Entry Draft">1988 NHL Entry Draft</a>, the Canucks chose winger <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Linden" title="Trevor Linden">Trevor Linden</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hockey_League" title="Western Hockey League">Western Hockey League</a> (WHL). The <a href="/wiki/1989_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="1989 NHL Entry Draft">following year</a>, the team made a controversial selection by choosing Russian winger <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Bure" title="Pavel Bure">Pavel Bure</a> 113th overall. Bure was believed by most teams to be ineligible for selection that year. Consequently, his draft by the Canucks took a year to be verified by the league as team management went about procuring documents to prove his eligibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the decade turned, a shift in the Canucks' leadership occurred as Stan Smyl resigned his captaincy prior to the <a href="/wiki/1990%E2%80%9391_NHL_season" title="1990–91 NHL season">1990–91 season</a> due to a reduced on-ice role with the team. In his place, the Canucks implemented a rotating captaincy of Linden, <a href="/wiki/Dan_Quinn_(ice_hockey)" title="Dan Quinn (ice hockey)">Dan Quinn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Doug_Lidster" title="Doug Lidster">Doug Lidster</a>; of the three, Linden retained the captaincy thereafter, becoming the youngest permanent captain in team history at 21 years of age. At the end of the season, Smyl retired as the team's all-time leader in games played, goals, assists and points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smyl_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-smyl-30">[30]</a></sup> Led by Linden and in large part to Quinn's dealings, the Canucks rose to prominence in the early 1990s. This increased success came roughly around the time the Oilers and Flames began to sink in the standings. As a result, Vancouver won their first division title in 17 years with 42 wins, 26 losses and 12 ties during the <a href="/wiki/1991%E2%80%9392_NHL_season" title="1991–92 NHL season">1991–92 season</a>. During the campaign, the Canucks honoured Smyl, who had remained on the team as an assistant coach, by making him the first player in team history to have his jersey (number 12) retired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smyl_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-smyl-30">[30]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/1992_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="1992 Stanley Cup playoffs">1992 playoffs</a>, the Canucks won their first series since 1982 before being eliminated by the Oilers in the second round.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-7"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup> Quinn and Bure became the first Canucks recipients of major NHL awards in the off-season, being awarded the <a href="/wiki/Jack_Adams_Award" title="Jack Adams Award">Jack Adams Award</a> as the best coach (Quinn assumed a dual coaching and general managerial role starting that year) and the top rookie in the league, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vault_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-vault-31">[31]</a></sup> The following year, the Canucks repeated as regular season division champions, while Bure emerged as arguably the team's first superstar with his first of back-to-back 60-goal seasons, totals which remain the highest recorded in Canucks history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-single-season_goals_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-single-season_goals-32">[32]</a></sup> As the team struggled to score in the second half of the <a href="/wiki/1993%E2%80%9394_Vancouver_Canucks_season" title="1993–94 Vancouver Canucks season">1993–94 season</a>, Bure recorded 49 goals in the club's final 51 games and contributed to 46.45% of his team's goals in the final 47 games of the season to carry the Canucks into the 1994 post-season. Jim Matheson of the <i><a href="/wiki/Edmonton_Journal" title="Edmonton Journal">Edmonton Journal</a></i> called Bure "the NHL's best forward the last 40 games, scoring almost a goal a game."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1994_Stanley_Cup_run">1994 Stanley Cup run</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vancouver_Canucks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1994 Stanley Cup run">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/1993%E2%80%9394_Vancouver_Canucks_season" title="1993–94 Vancouver Canucks season">1993–94 Vancouver Canucks season</a> and <a href="/wiki/1994_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1994 Stanley Cup Finals">1994 Stanley Cup Finals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kirk_McLean.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="604" data-file-width="440" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Kirk_McLean.jpg/200px-Kirk_McLean.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Kirk_McLean.jpg/300px-Kirk_McLean.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Kirk_McLean.jpg/400px-Kirk_McLean.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kirk_McLean.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Kirk_McLean" title="Kirk McLean">Kirk McLean</a> was a key member of the Canucks' 1994 Stanley Cup run.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1994_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1994 Stanley Cup Finals">1994</a>, the Canucks made their second trip to the Stanley Cup Finals, entering the <a href="/wiki/1994_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="1994 Stanley Cup playoffs">playoffs</a> as the seventh <a href="/wiki/Seed_(sports)" title="Seed (sports)">seed</a> in the renamed <a href="/wiki/Western_Conference_(NHL)" title="Western Conference (NHL)">Western Conference</a>. Despite underachieving in the regular season (their points total decreased by 16 from the previous year),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seasons_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-seasons-14">[14]</a></sup> the Canucks played well in the playoffs and embarked on another unexpected run.</p>
<p>Opening the playoffs with a close first-round series against the Calgary Flames, Vancouver rallied from a three-games-to-one deficit to win the series in seven contests. Games 5 through 7 were all won in overtime with goals from <a href="/wiki/Geoff_Courtnall" title="Geoff Courtnall">Geoff Courtnall</a>, Trevor Linden and Pavel Bure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The deciding seventh game featured two of the most recognizable and celebrated plays in Canucks history. With the game tied 3–3 in the first overtime, goaltender Kirk McLean made what became known thereafter as "The Save", sliding across the crease feet-first and stacking his pads on the goal line to stop <a href="/wiki/Robert_Reichel" title="Robert Reichel">Robert Reichel</a> on a one-timer pass from <a href="/wiki/Theoren_Fleury" title="Theoren Fleury">Theoren Fleury</a>. The following period, Pavel Bure received a breakaway pass from defenceman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jeff_Brown_(ice_hockey_b._1966)" title="Jeff Brown (ice hockey b. 1966)">Jeff Brown</a> before deking Calgary goaltender <a href="/wiki/Mike_Vernon_(ice_hockey)" title="Mike Vernon (ice hockey)">Mike Vernon</a> to score and win the series. Fifteen years later, Bure's goal and McLean's save were ranked first and second in a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vancouver_Sun" title="Vancouver Sun">Vancouver Sun</a></i> article listing the "40 most memorable moments in team history."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forty_memorable_moments_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-forty_memorable_moments-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following their victory over the Flames, the Canucks then went on to defeat both the <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Stars" title="Dallas Stars">Dallas Stars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Maple_Leafs" title="Toronto Maple Leafs">Toronto Maple Leafs</a> (both in five games) en route to the franchise's second Stanley Cup Finals appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-8"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup> Forward Greg Adams sent the Canucks into the Finals with a double-overtime goal against Maple Leafs goaltender <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_Potvin" title="Félix Potvin">Felix Potvin</a> in Game 5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forty_memorable_moments_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-forty_memorable_moments-35">[35]</a></sup> Staging the second coast-to-coast Finals in league history, the Canucks were matched against the <a href="/wiki/Presidents%27_Trophy" title="Presidents' Trophy">Presidents' Trophy</a>-winning <a href="/wiki/New_York_Rangers" title="New York Rangers">New York Rangers</a>. Vancouver achieved victory in Game 1 by a score of 3–2 in overtime, largely due to a 52-save performance by goaltender McLean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> After losing Games 2, 3 and 4, the Canucks won the next two to force a seventh game at <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a> on June 14, 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-9"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup> Despite a two-goal effort (one on a shorthanded breakaway) from Linden (who was playing with cracked ribs),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WeekesBanks2010_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-WeekesBanks2010-37">[37]</a></sup> Vancouver lost the game by a 3–2 score. The Canucks' efforts to tie the game included a post hit by forward <a href="/wiki/Nathan_LaFayette" title="Nathan LaFayette">Nathan LaFayette</a> with just over a minute remaining in regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rossiter_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rossiter-38">[38]</a></sup> The loss was followed by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1994_Stanley_Cup_riot" title="1994 Stanley Cup riot">riot</a> in Downtown Vancouver, which resulted in property damage, injuries and arrests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Two days after the riots, the team held a rally at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BC_Place_Stadium" title="BC Place Stadium">BC Place</a> attended by 45,000 fans, who congratulated the team for their effort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1994.E2.80.932001:_25th_Anniversary.2C_new_arena.2C_and_a_new_beginning">1994–2001: 25th Anniversary, new arena, and a new beginning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vancouver_Canucks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1994–2001: 25th Anniversary, new arena, and a new beginning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With a young core that included Linden, Bure and McLean still in their 20s after the 1994 playoffs, the Canucks appeared poised to remain contenders in the league.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rossiter_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rossiter-38">[38]</a></sup> However, the team failed to record a winning season in the six years following their Stanley Cup Finals appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seasons_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-seasons-14">[14]</a></sup> Prior to the lockout-shortened <a href="/wiki/1994%E2%80%9395_NHL_season" title="1994–95 NHL season">1994–95 season</a>, Quinn stepped down as head coach to focus on his managerial duties and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Rick_Ley" title="Rick Ley">Rick Ley</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Vancouver finished with a .500 record that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seasons_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-seasons-14">[14]</a></sup> Their elimination from the <a href="/wiki/1995_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="1995 Stanley Cup playoffs">1995 Stanley Cup playoffs</a> in Game 4 of the second round marked the Canucks' last game played at the Pacific Coliseum,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-10"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> as the team moved into the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Motors_Place" title="General Motors Place">General Motors Place</a> (since renamed Rogers Arena), a new $160 million arena situated in <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Vancouver" title="Downtown Vancouver">Downtown Vancouver</a>, the following season.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GM_Place_1997.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1252" data-file-width="1890" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/GM_Place_1997.jpg/280px-GM_Place_1997.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/GM_Place_1997.jpg/420px-GM_Place_1997.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/GM_Place_1997.jpg/560px-GM_Place_1997.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GM_Place_1997.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Vancouver Canucks and the Edmonton Oilers warm up before a match at the new General Motors Place, October 1997.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Canucks made another significant move in the off-season by acquiring high-scoring Russian forward <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Mogilny" title="Alexander Mogilny">Alexander Mogilny</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Buffalo_Sabres" title="Buffalo Sabres">Buffalo Sabres</a>, reuniting Bure with his former <a href="/wiki/HC_CSKA_Moscow" title="HC CSKA Moscow">CSKA Moscow</a> and national team linemate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> While Mogilny became the second player in team history to record 50 goals and 100 points in a season,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-single-season_goals_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-single-season_goals-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-single-season_points_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-single-season_points-44">[44]</a></sup> chiefly playing with centre <a href="/wiki/Cliff_Ronning" title="Cliff Ronning">Cliff Ronning</a>, the expected chemistry between Mogilny and Bure never materialized, with the latter suffering a season-ending knee injury early in the campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Vancouver finished <a href="/wiki/1995%E2%80%9396_NHL_season" title="1995–96 NHL season">1995–96</a> two games below .500 and were defeated in the first round of the playoffs by the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Avalanche" title="Colorado Avalanche">Colorado Avalanche</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-11"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seasons_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-seasons-14">[14]</a></sup> The season also marked the arrival of another future Canucks superstar, as <a href="/wiki/Markus_N%C3%A4slund" title="Markus Näslund">Markus Naslund</a> was acquired from the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Penguins" title="Pittsburgh Penguins">Pittsburgh Penguins</a> in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Alek_Stojanov" title="Alek Stojanov">Alek Stojanov</a>. The deal is regarded as one of the most lopsided trades in NHL history, as Stojanov soon became a minor-leaguer, while Naslund became the team's all-time leading goal- and point-scorer years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1996 off-season, Ley was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Tom_Renney" title="Tom Renney">Tom Renney</a>, who lasted for less than two seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Despite strong performances from Mogilny and team-leading point-scorer <a href="/wiki/Martin_G%C3%A9linas" title="Martin Gélinas">Martin Gelinas</a> in Bure and Linden's absence (both of whom were injured for long periods of time during the season),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seasons_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-seasons-14">[14]</a></sup> the Canucks missed the playoffs for the first of four consecutive seasons that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-12"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup> Making another high-profile acquisition on July 27, 1997, the Canucks signed free agent <a href="/wiki/Mark_Messier" title="Mark Messier">Mark Messier</a> to a three-year deal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klein2004_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klein2004-51">[51]</a></sup> They had come close to signing <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Gretzky" title="Wayne Gretzky">Wayne Gretzky</a> the previous summer, but were reportedly spurned away when they refused to continue negotiations and gave Gretzky an ultimatum to sign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quinn_fired_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-quinn_fired-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:207px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marccrawford_2006nhlawards.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1750" data-file-width="1150" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Marccrawford_2006nhlawards.jpg/205px-Marccrawford_2006nhlawards.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Marccrawford_2006nhlawards.jpg/308px-Marccrawford_2006nhlawards.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Marccrawford_2006nhlawards.jpg/410px-Marccrawford_2006nhlawards.jpg 2x" width="205"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marccrawford_2006nhlawards.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Marc Crawford became the Canucks' head coach in 1998–99. Crawford also played for the team in the 1980s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Heading into the <a href="/wiki/1997%E2%80%9398_NHL_season" title="1997–98 NHL season">1997–98 season</a>, Linden resigned his captaincy for Messier, who had developed a strong reputation as a leader, having captained the New York Rangers over the Canucks in 1994 (he also captained the Oilers to a Stanley Cup in 1990). Linden later recalled regretting the decision, feeling that Messier generated hostility and tension in the dressing room.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Messier later said if he could change one thing about his time in Vancouver, he would not have accepted the captaincy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Canucks began the campaign overseas in a two-game series against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mighty_Ducks_of_Anaheim" title="Mighty Ducks of Anaheim">Mighty Ducks of Anaheim</a> in Tokyo, Japan. It marked the first time in NHL history that a regular season game was held outside of North America – an effort from the league to attract attention to the sport in anticipation of the <a href="/wiki/1998_Winter_Olympics" title="1998 Winter Olympics">1998 Winter Olympics</a>, which were held in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nagano" title="Nagano">Nagano</a>, Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> As the team's performance continued to worsen, starting the <a href="/wiki/1997%E2%80%9398_NHL_season" title="1997–98 NHL season">1997–98 season</a> with three wins in the first 16 games, Quinn was fired as general manager after ten years with the team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quinn_fired_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-quinn_fired-52">[52]</a></sup> Soon thereafter, Renney was fired and replaced as coach by <a href="/wiki/Mike_Keenan" title="Mike Keenan">Mike Keenan</a>, reuniting him with Messier, another central figure from the Rangers' 1994 team. Keenan's hiring reportedly exacerbated tensions between groups of Canucks players and his negative relationship with Linden was given ample media attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Captain_of_Islanders_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Captain_of_Islanders-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Darkest_time_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Darkest_time-57">[57]</a></sup> Two months into his tenure with the team, his role was expanded and he was made de facto general manager. With control of player personnel, Keenan overhauled the roster, making ten trades within two months, most notably dealing Linden to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Islanders" title="New York Islanders">New York Islanders</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keenan_fired_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-keenan_fired-58">[58]</a></sup> Although the trade was unpopular with fans, the Canucks received winger <a href="/wiki/Todd_Bertuzzi" title="Todd Bertuzzi">Todd Bertuzzi</a> in return, who would later become an integral part of the team's return to success in the next decade. Defenceman <a href="/wiki/Bryan_McCabe" title="Bryan McCabe">Bryan McCabe</a> was also part of the deal, who would eventually be involved in a key transaction in the <a href="/wiki/1999_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="1999 NHL Entry Draft">1999 NHL Entry Draft</a>. After the Canucks finished the 1997–98 season last in the Western Conference,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> former NHL Vice President <a href="/wiki/Brian_Burke_(ice_hockey)" title="Brian Burke (ice hockey)">Brian Burke</a> was named general manager in the summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Suffering their worst season since 1977–78 the <a href="/wiki/1998%E2%80%9399_NHL_season" title="1998–99 NHL season">subsequent year</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seasons_14-8"><a href="#cite_note-seasons-14">[14]</a></sup> Keenan was fired midway through and replaced with <a href="/wiki/Marc_Crawford" title="Marc Crawford">Marc Crawford</a> (who had won the Stanley Cup with the Colorado Avalanche in <a href="/wiki/1996_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1996 Stanley Cup Finals">1996</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keenan_fired_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-keenan_fired-58">[58]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Pavel Bure, unhappy in Vancouver, had withheld himself from the team and requested a trade at the beginning of the campaign. By January 1999, he was dealt to the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Panthers" title="Florida Panthers">Florida Panthers</a> in a seven-player trade, which saw eventual five-time NHL All-Star <a href="/wiki/Ed_Jovanovski" title="Ed Jovanovski">Ed Jovanovski</a> heading west. The trade also involved two draft picks. Finishing last in the Western Conference for a second-straight year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Vancouver possessed the fourth overall pick in the 1999 NHL Entry Draft. Set on drafting highly touted Swedish forwards <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Sedin" title="Daniel Sedin">Daniel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henrik_Sedin" title="Henrik Sedin">Henrik Sedin</a>, Burke orchestrated several transactions to move up to the second and third overall picks, with which he chose both players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Canucks began to show improvement in the <a href="/wiki/1999%E2%80%932000_NHL_season" title="1999–2000 NHL season">1999–2000 season</a>, finishing four points out of a playoff spot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> During the campaign, Mogilny was traded to the <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Devils" title="New Jersey Devils">New Jersey Devils</a> for forwards <a href="/wiki/Denis_Pederson" title="Denis Pederson">Denis Pederson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brendan_Morrison" title="Brendan Morrison">Brendan Morrison</a>. With Bure gone and Messier in the last year of his contract, several previously under-achieving players began developing into key contributors for the team, most notably Markus Naslund and Todd Bertuzzi. In the off-season, Messier left the team and returned to the Rangers; during the team's September 2000 training camp, held in Sweden, Naslund was selected to replace Messier as captain, a position he held for eight years, tying Smyl's record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-longest_captaincy_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-longest_captaincy-20">[20]</a></sup> As part of the team's stay in Sweden, they played exhibition games against Swedish and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finnish</a> teams as part of the <a href="/wiki/NHL_Challenge" title="NHL Challenge">NHL Challenge</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2001.E2.80.932005:_.22West_Coast_Express.22_years">2001–2005: "West Coast Express" years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vancouver_Canucks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title='Edit section: 2001–2005: "West Coast Express" years'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Under the leadership of General Manager Brian Burke and Head Coach Marc Crawford,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thornton2010_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thornton2010-64">[64]</a></sup> the Canucks once again became a playoff team. After qualifying for the post-season in <a href="/wiki/2001_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="2001 Stanley Cup playoffs">2001</a> and <a href="/wiki/2002_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="2002 Stanley Cup playoffs">2002</a> as the eighth and final seed in the Western Conference (losing to the eventual Stanley Cup winners Colorado Avalanche and <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Red_Wings" title="Detroit Red Wings">Detroit Red Wings</a>, respectively),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-13"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup> the Canucks became regular contenders for the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Division_(NHL)" title="Northwest Division (NHL)">Northwest Division</a> title.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:West_Coast_Express_line.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="5243" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/West_Coast_Express_line.jpg/350px-West_Coast_Express_line.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/West_Coast_Express_line.jpg/525px-West_Coast_Express_line.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/West_Coast_Express_line.jpg/700px-West_Coast_Express_line.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:West_Coast_Express_line.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Markus Naslund, Brendan Morrison and Todd Bertuzzi formed the "West Coast Express" line.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Coinciding with the team's success in the early 2000s was the rise of captain Markus Naslund and power forward Todd Bertuzzi into high-scoring wingers and NHL All-Stars. Joined by centre Brendan Morrison during the <a href="/wiki/2001%E2%80%9302_NHL_season" title="2001–02 NHL season">2001–02 season</a>, the trio were nicknamed the "<a href="/wiki/West_Coast_Express_(ice_hockey)" title="West Coast Express (ice hockey)">West Coast Express</a>" (after the Vancouver <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_Express" title="West Coast Express">rail service of the same name</a>) among Canucks fans and media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Over the next three years, Naslund ranked in the top five among league scorers and was a <a href="/wiki/Ted_Lindsay_Award" title="Ted Lindsay Award">Lester B. Pearson Award</a> winner and <a href="/wiki/Hart_Memorial_Trophy" title="Hart Memorial Trophy">Hart Memorial Trophy</a> finalist in <a href="/wiki/2002%E2%80%9303_NHL_season" title="2002–03 NHL season">2003</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2002_nhl_leading_scorers_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-2002_nhl_leading_scorers-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2003_nhl_leading_scorers_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-2003_nhl_leading_scorers-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Bertuzzi was also a top-five scorer in the league in 2001–02 and 2002–03.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2002_nhl_leading_scorers_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-2002_nhl_leading_scorers-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2003_nhl_leading_scorers_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-2003_nhl_leading_scorers-67">[67]</a></sup> During this span, Burke made a trade with the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Capitals" title="Washington Capitals">Washington Capitals</a> to facilitate the return of Trevor Linden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The ex-captain returned to a markedly different Canucks team with a young core consisting of the aforementioned trio, defencemen Ed Jovanovski and <a href="/wiki/Mattias_%C3%96hlund" title="Mattias Öhlund">Mattias Ohlund</a>, as well as goaltender <a href="/wiki/Dan_Cloutier" title="Dan Cloutier">Dan Cloutier</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2002–03, the Canucks lost the division title to the Colorado Avalanche on the last day of the regular season. Individually, Naslund was surpassed the same night by Avalanche forwards <a href="/wiki/Peter_Forsberg" title="Peter Forsberg">Peter Forsberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Milan_Hejduk" title="Milan Hejduk">Milan Hejduk</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Art_Ross_Trophy" title="Art Ross Trophy">Art Ross Trophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maurice_%22Rocket%22_Richard_Trophy" title='Maurice "Rocket" Richard Trophy'>Maurice "Rocket" Richard Trophy</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-choked_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-choked-72">[72]</a></sup> Entering the <a href="/wiki/2003_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="2003 Stanley Cup playoffs">2003 playoffs</a> with the fourth seed in the West, the Canucks won their first playoff series in eight years, defeating the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Blues" title="St. Louis Blues">St. Louis Blues</a> in seven games before losing to the <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_Wild" title="Minnesota Wild">Minnesota Wild</a> in the second round.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-14"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Amidst a run for the team's first Northwest Division title the <a href="/wiki/2003%E2%80%9304_NHL_season" title="2003–04 NHL season">following season</a>, the Canucks received significant media attention for their involvement in a violent on-ice attack during a game against the Avalanche. On March 8, 2004, Bertuzzi grabbed Avalanche forward <a href="/wiki/Steve_Moore_(ice_hockey)" title="Steve Moore (ice hockey)">Steve Moore</a> from behind and punched him in the head. As Moore fell to the ice, Bertuzzi landed on top of him; Moore suffered three fractured neck vertebrae, facial cuts and a concussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Bertuzzi's actions were in retaliation of a hit that Moore landed on Naslund during a previous game between the two teams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> For his actions, Bertuzzi was suspended by the NHL and <a href="/wiki/International_Ice_Hockey_Federation" title="International Ice Hockey Federation">International Ice Hockey Federation</a> (IIHF) through to the start of the <a href="/wiki/2005%E2%80%9306_NHL_season" title="2005–06 NHL season">2005–06 season</a>. He also faced legal action in <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_British_Columbia" title="Supreme Court of British Columbia">British Columbia court</a>, while Moore filed lawsuits against him and the Canucks organization in <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Supreme_Court" title="Colorado Supreme Court">Colorado</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Court_of_Justice" title="Ontario Court of Justice">Ontario</a> courts.</p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Todd_Bertuzzi%E2%80%93Steve_Moore_incident" title="Todd Bertuzzi–Steve Moore incident">Todd Bertuzzi–Steve Moore incident</a></div>
<p>The Canucks went on to win their first Northwest Division title that season, but lost in the first round of the <a href="/wiki/2004_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="2004 Stanley Cup playoffs">2004 playoffs</a> to the Calgary Flames.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-15"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup> After their elimination, Burke's contract as general manager was not renewed and he was replaced by Assistant General Manager and Director of Hockey Operations <a href="/wiki/Dave_Nonis" title="Dave Nonis">Dave Nonis</a>. At 37 years of age, he became the youngest general manager in team history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Due to the <a href="/wiki/2004%E2%80%9305_NHL_lockout" title="2004–05 NHL lockout">NHL lockout</a>, the 2004–05 season was not played. Several Canucks players went overseas to Europe to play professionally, including Naslund and the Sedin twins, who all returned to their former Swedish team, <a href="/wiki/Modo_Hockey" title="Modo Hockey">Modo Hockey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2005.E2.80.932010:_Post-lockout">2005–2010: Post-lockout</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vancouver_Canucks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 2005–2010: Post-lockout">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:237px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Todd_Bertuzzi_and_Dan_Cloutier_2005.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="853" height="353" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Todd_Bertuzzi_and_Dan_Cloutier_2005.jpg/235px-Todd_Bertuzzi_and_Dan_Cloutier_2005.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Todd_Bertuzzi_and_Dan_Cloutier_2005.jpg/353px-Todd_Bertuzzi_and_Dan_Cloutier_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Todd_Bertuzzi_and_Dan_Cloutier_2005.jpg/470px-Todd_Bertuzzi_and_Dan_Cloutier_2005.jpg 2x" width="235"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Todd_Bertuzzi_and_Dan_Cloutier_2005.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Todd Bertuzzi and Dan Cloutier of the Vancouver Canucks leave the ice after warmup for a match in 2005. 2005–06 was their final season as members of the team.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Upon the resolution of the labour dispute between NHL players and owners, new gameplay rules were set in place for the <a href="/wiki/2005%E2%80%9306_NHL_season" title="2005–06 NHL season">2005–06 season</a> that were intended to benefit skilled players and generate more goal-scoring. As the Canucks' basis of success in previous seasons was built on playing a fast-paced, high-scoring style of play, expectations for the team were high going into the season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[notes 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thrive_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-thrive-79">[78]</a></sup> However, the team failed to qualify for the playoffs, completing the regular season ninth place in the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> The first line of Naslund, Bertuzzi and Morrison suffered offensively, as all three players recorded decreased points totals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seasons_14-9"><a href="#cite_note-seasons-14">[14]</a></sup> Head Coach Marc Crawford later recalled the campaign as a turning point for the team's offensive leadership as the Sedin twins began their rise to stardom, matching the top line's production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-surprise_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-surprise-81">[80]</a></sup> Crawford was fired in the off-season and replaced with <a href="/wiki/Alain_Vigneault" title="Alain Vigneault">Alain Vigneault</a>, who had been coach of the team's <a href="/wiki/American_Hockey_League" title="American Hockey League">American Hockey League</a> (AHL) affiliate, the <a href="/wiki/Manitoba_Moose" title="Manitoba Moose">Manitoba Moose</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> Three days after Vigneault's hiring, Nonis dealt Bertuzzi to the Florida Panthers, ending the "West Coast Express" era. (Naslund and Morrison would leave the team two years later.) In return, the Canucks received All-Star goaltender <a href="/wiki/Roberto_Luongo" title="Roberto Luongo">Roberto Luongo</a> as part of a six-player trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> With the acquisition of Luongo, Cloutier was traded away to the Los Angeles Kings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>With widespread changes to team personnel in <a href="/wiki/2006%E2%80%9307_NHL_season" title="2006–07 NHL season">2006–07</a>, the Canucks won the Northwest Division title for the second time in three seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seasons_14-10"><a href="#cite_note-seasons-14">[14]</a></sup> In his first season with the Canucks, Luongo was nominated for the Hart Memorial and <a href="/wiki/Vezina_Trophy" title="Vezina Trophy">Vezina</a> Trophies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007_nhl_awards_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-2007_nhl_awards-85">[84]</a></sup> He also tied <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Parent" title="Bernie Parent">Bernie Parent</a> for the second-most wins in a single-season by an NHL goaltender, with 47.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> The Canucks opened the <a href="/wiki/2007_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="2007 Stanley Cup playoffs">2007 playoffs</a> with a quadruple-overtime win against the <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Stars" title="Dallas Stars">Dallas Stars</a>. Ending at the 138-minute mark, the game was the longest in club history and the sixth-longest in NHL history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> The Canucks also set a league record for shots against in one game, allowing 76.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> Vancouver won the series in seven games despite a lack of goal-scoring; Stars goaltender <a href="/wiki/Marty_Turco" title="Marty Turco">Marty Turco</a> recorded three shutouts in the series, becoming the only goalie to achieve the feat and still lose a series.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Advancing to the second round, the team was defeated in five games by the Anaheim Ducks, who went on to win the Stanley Cup <a href="/wiki/2007_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="2007 Stanley Cup Finals">that year</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> Following the playoffs, Head Coach Vigneault received the <a href="/wiki/Jack_Adams_Award" title="Jack Adams Award">Jack Adams Award</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007_nhl_awards_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-2007_nhl_awards-85">[84]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Towelpower.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="453" data-file-width="604" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Towelpower.jpg/250px-Towelpower.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Towelpower.jpg/375px-Towelpower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Towelpower.jpg/500px-Towelpower.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Towelpower.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Towel_Power" title="Towel Power">Towel Power</a> in the 2007 Playoffs.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Suffering numerous injuries to players in the <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%9308_NHL_season" title="2007–08 NHL season">2007–08 season</a>, the Canucks struggled and finished three points out of a playoff spot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> The final game of the season, a 7–1 loss to the Calgary Flames, marked Trevor Linden's last NHL game, as the former Canucks' all-time leading scorer retired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> Having missed the playoffs for the second time in three years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-playoff_history_13-16"><a href="#cite_note-playoff_history-13">[13]</a></sup> the team underwent numerous personnel changes in the off-season. After Nonis was fired and replaced with former player agent <a href="/wiki/Mike_Gillis" title="Mike Gillis">Mike Gillis</a> in April 2008,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> longtime Canucks captain Markus Naslund, as well as Brendan Morrison, were let go via free agency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> Also in the off-season, on May 29, 2008, the Canucks lost defensive prospect <a href="/wiki/Luc_Bourdon" title="Luc Bourdon">Luc Bourdon</a> to a fatal motorcycle crash near his hometown of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shippagan,_New_Brunswick" title="Shippagan, New Brunswick">Shippagan, New Brunswick</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>With Naslund's departure, Gillis announced on September 30, 2008, that Luongo had been named team captain, marking the first time since <a href="/wiki/Bill_Durnan" title="Bill Durnan">Bill Durnan</a> of the Montreal Canadiens in <a href="/wiki/1947%E2%80%9348_NHL_season" title="1947–48 NHL season">1947</a> that a goaltender had been named the captain of an NHL team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> During the ensuing season, the Canucks retired their second jersey number in team history, hanging Linden's number 16 beside Smyl's number 12 in a pre-game ceremony on December 17, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> Later that month, the Canucks acquired unrestricted free agent <a href="/wiki/Mats_Sundin" title="Mats Sundin">Mats Sundin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> The arrival of the former Toronto Maple Leafs captain and 500-goal scorer in the NHL came with high expectations. However, Sundin scored at a pace below his usual pace and retired in the subsequent off-season. The team finished the regular season with another Northwest Division title and the third seed in the Western Conference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2009_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="2009 Stanley Cup playoffs">2009 playoffs</a>, the Canucks swept their first round series against the St. Louis Blues (the first four-game sweep in franchise history),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> but were defeated in six games by the Chicago Blackhawks in the second round.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/2009%E2%80%9310_NHL_season" title="2009–10 NHL season">2009–10 season</a>, the Canucks faced the longest road trip in NHL history, with 14 games over six weeks, from January 27 to March 13, 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> The scheduling was a result of Vancouver hosting the <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Olympics" title="2010 Winter Olympics">2010 Winter Olympics</a>, which shut down the NHL for two weeks, facilitating General Motors Place's use for <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey_at_the_2010_Winter_Olympics" title="Ice hockey at the 2010 Winter Olympics">ice hockey during the games</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> It marked the first time that an NHL market hosted an <a href="/wiki/Winter_Olympic_Games" title="Winter Olympic Games">Olympics</a> since the league allowed its players to compete in the games, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/1998_Winter_Olympics" title="1998 Winter Olympics">1998 Games</a> in Nagano. Among the several Canucks players named to their respective national teams, centre <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Kesler" title="Ryan Kesler">Ryan Kesler</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="United States men's national ice hockey team">United States</a> and goaltender Roberto Luongo of <a href="/wiki/Canada_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Canada men's national ice hockey team">Canada</a> played against each other in the gold medal game; Luongo and Team Canada emerged with the win.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the NHL season resumed, Henrik Sedin went on to become the first Canucks player to win the Art Ross and Hart Memorial Trophies as the league's leading scorer and most valuable player, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> He achieved the feat with a franchise-record 112 points, surpassing Pavel Bure's mark of 110 set in 1991–92.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-single-season_points_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-single-season_points-44">[44]</a></sup> Vancouver won the Northwest Division title and finished third in the Western Conference for the second-straight year. They opened the playoffs by defeating the sixth-place Los Angeles Kings in six games, but were once again eliminated by Chicago, who went on to win the Stanley Cup <a href="/wiki/2010_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="2010 Stanley Cup Finals">that year</a>, the following round in six games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010.E2.80.932011:_40th_anniversary">2010–2011: 40th anniversary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vancouver_Canucks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 2010–2011: 40th anniversary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
